<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_iodine" title="Template:Infobox iodine"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_iodine" title="Template talk:Infobox iodine"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_iodine&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Iodine</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>I</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 53. The heaviest of the stable <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, it exists as a lustrous, purple-black non-metallic solid at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_condition" title="Standard condition">standard conditions</a> that <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimes</a> readily to form a violet gas.  The elemental form was discovered by the French chemist <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Courtois" title="Bernard Courtois">Bernard Courtois</a> in 1811. It was named two years later by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph-Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph-Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph-Louis Gay-Lussac</a> from this property, after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>ἰώδης</i> "violet-coloured".
</p><p>Iodine occurs in many oxidation states, including <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> (I<sup>−</sup>), <a href="/wiki/Iodate" title="Iodate">iodate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), and the various <a href="/wiki/Periodate" title="Periodate">periodate</a> anions. It is the least abundant of the stable halogens, being the sixty-first most abundant element.  It is even less abundant than the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_earth_elements" title="Rare earth elements">rare earths</a>. It is the heaviest essential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineral_nutrient" title="Mineral nutrient">mineral nutrient</a>. Iodine is essential in the synthesis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_hormone" title="Thyroid hormone">thyroid hormones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">Iodine deficiency</a> affects about two billion people and is the leading preventable cause of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intellectual_disabilities" title="Intellectual disabilities">intellectual disabilities</a>.
</p><p>The dominant producers of iodine today are <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. Iodine and its <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a> are primarily used in <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a>. Due to its high atomic number and ease of attachment to <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>, it has also found favour as a non-toxic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiocontrast" title="Radiocontrast">radiocontrast</a> material. Because of the specificity of its uptake by the human body, radioactive isotopes of iodine can also be used to treat <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">thyroid cancer</a>. Iodine is also used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> in the industrial production of <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1811, iodine was discovered by French chemist <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Courtois" title="Bernard Courtois">Bernard Courtois</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-court_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-court-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> who was born to a manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">saltpeter</a> (an essential component of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>). At the time of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, saltpeter was in great demand in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. Saltpeter produced from French <a href="/wiki/Nitrary" title="Nitrary">nitre beds</a> required <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a>, which could be isolated from <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a> collected on the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>. To isolate the sodium carbonate, seaweed was burned and the ash washed with water. The remaining waste was destroyed by adding <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>. Courtois once added excessive sulfuric acid and a cloud of purple vapour rose. He noted that the vapour crystallised on cold surfaces, making dark crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood794_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood794-7">[7]</a></sup> Courtois suspected that this material was a new element but lacked funding to pursue it further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vdK_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-vdK-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Courtois gave samples to his friends, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bernard_Desormes" title="Charles Bernard Desormes">Charles Bernard Desormes</a> (1777–1838) and <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Cl%C3%A9ment" title="Nicolas Clément">Nicolas Clément</a> (1779–1841), to continue research. He also gave some of the substance to <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> (1778–1850), and to <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="André-Marie Ampère">André-Marie Ampère</a> (1775–1836). On 29 November 1813, Desormes and Clément made Courtois' discovery public. They described the substance to a meeting of the Imperial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institute_of_France" title="Institute of France">Institute of France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> On 6 December, Gay-Lussac announced that the new substance was either an element or a compound of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gay-Lussac_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gay-Lussac-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> It was Gay-Lussac who suggested the name <i>"iode"</i>, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Greek</a> word <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἰοειδής</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> (<i>ioeidēs</i>) for violet (because of the colour of iodine vapor).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-court_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-court-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gay-Lussac_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gay-Lussac-10">[10]</a></sup> Ampère had given some of his sample to English chemist <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> (1778–1829), who experimented on the substance and noted its similarity to <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Davy sent a letter dated 10 December to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_London" title="Royal Society of London">Royal Society of London</a> stating that he had identified a new element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Arguments erupted between Davy and Gay-Lussac over who identified iodine first, but both scientists acknowledged Courtois as the first to isolate the element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vdK_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-vdK-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IodoAtomico.JPG"><img alt="Round bottom flask filled with violet iodine vapour" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="252" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/IodoAtomico.JPG/150px-IodoAtomico.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/IodoAtomico.JPG/225px-IodoAtomico.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/IodoAtomico.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IodoAtomico.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Violet iodine vapour in a round-bottomed flask</div></div></div>
<p>Iodine is the fourth <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogen</a>, being a member of group 17 in the periodic table, below <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>; it is the heaviest stable member of its group. (The scarce and fugitive fifth halogen, the radioactive <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">astatine</a>, is not well-studied due to its expense and inaccessibility in large quantities, but appears to show various unusual properties due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_effects" title="Relativistic effects">relativistic effects</a>.) Iodine has an electron configuration of [Kr]4d<sup>10</sup>5s<sup>2</sup>5p<sup>5</sup>, with the seven electrons in the fifth and outermost shell being its <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>. Like the other halogens, it is one electron short of a full octet and is hence a strong oxidising agent, reacting with many elements in order to complete its outer shell, although in keeping with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_trend" title="Periodic trend">periodic trends</a>, it is the weakest oxidising agent among the stable halogens: it has the lowest <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> among them, just 2.66 on the Pauling scale (compare fluorine, chlorine, and bromine at 3.98, 3.16, and 2.96 respectively; astatine continues the trend with an electronegativity of 2.2). Elemental iodine hence forms <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic molecules</a> with chemical formula I<sub>2</sub>, where two iodine atoms share a pair of electrons in order to each achieve a stable octet for themselves; at high temperatures, these diatomic molecules reversibly dissociate a pair of iodine atoms. Similarly, the iodide anion, I<sup>−</sup>, is the strongest reducing agent among the stable halogens, being the most easily oxidised back to diatomic I<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup> (Astatine goes further, being indeed unstable as At<sup>−</sup> and readily oxidised to At<sup>0</sup> or At<sup>+</sup>, although the existence of At<sub>2</sub> is not settled.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The halogens darken in colour as the group is descended: fluorine is a very pale yellow gas, chlorine is greenish-yellow, and bromine is a reddish-brown volatile liquid. Iodine conforms to the prevailing trend, being a shiny black crystalline solid that melts at 114 °C and boils at 183 °C to form a violet gas. This trend occurs because the wavelengths of visible light absorbed by the halogens increase down the group (though astatine may not conform to it, depending on how metallic it turns out to be).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup> Specifically, the violet colour of iodine gas results from the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_electron_transition" title="Atomic electron transition">electron transition</a> between the <a href="/wiki/HOMO/LUMO" title="HOMO/LUMO">highest occupied</a> antibonding <i>π<sub>g</sub></i> molecular orbital and the lowest vacant antibonding <i>σ<sub>u</sub></i> molecular orbital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood804_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood804-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elemental iodine is slightly soluble in water, with one gram dissolving in 3450 ml at 20 °C and 1280 ml at 50 °C; <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a> may be added to increase solubility via formation of <a href="/wiki/Triiodide" title="Triiodide">triiodide</a> ions, among other polyiodides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood804_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood804-18">[18]</a></sup> Nonpolar solvents such as <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a> provide a higher solubility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Polar solutions, such as aqueous solutions, are brown, reflecting the role of these solvents as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_base" title="Lewis base">Lewis bases</a>; on the other hand, nonpolar solutions are violet, the color of iodine vapour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood804_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood804-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charge-transfer_complex" title="Charge-transfer complex">Charge-transfer complexes</a> form when iodine is dissolved in polar solvents, hence changing the colour. Iodine is violet when dissolved in carbon tetrachloride and saturated hydrocarbons but deep brown in <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>, solvents that form charge-transfer adducts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2650" data-file-width="6220" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg/400px-Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg/600px-Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg/800px-Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iodine-triphenylphosphine_charge-transfer_complex_in_dichloromethane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>I<sub>2</sub>•<a href="/wiki/Triphenylphosphine" title="Triphenylphosphine">PPh<sub>3</sub></a> charge-transfer complexes in <a href="/wiki/Dichloromethane" title="Dichloromethane">CH<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub></a>. From left to right: (1) I<sub>2</sub> dissolved in dichloromethane – no CT complex. (2) A few seconds after excess PPh<sub>3</sub> was added – CT complex is forming. (3) One minute later after excess PPh<sub>3</sub> was added, the CT complex [Ph<sub>3</sub>PI]<sup>+</sup>I<sup>−</sup> has been formed. (4) Immediately after excess I<sub>2</sub> was added, which contains [Ph<sub>3</sub>PI]<sup>+</sup>[I<sub>3</sub>]<sup>−</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InorgChem_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-InorgChem-21">[21]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The melting and boiling points of iodine are the highest among the halogens, conforming to the increasing trend down the group, since iodine has the largest electron cloud among them that is the most easily polarised, resulting in its molecules having the strongest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_interaction" title="Van der Waals interaction">van der Waals interactions</a> among the halogens. Similarly, iodine is the least volatile of the halogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup> Because it has the largest <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">atomic radius</a> among the halogens, iodine has the lowest first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionisation_energy" title="Ionisation energy">ionisation energy</a>, lowest <a href="/wiki/Electron_affinity" title="Electron affinity">electron affinity</a>, lowest <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> and lowest reactivity of the halogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="927" data-file-width="1100" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png/150px-Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png/225px-Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png/300px-Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iodine-unit-cell-3D-balls-B.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of solid iodine</div></div></div>
<p>The interhalogen bond in diiodine is the weakest of all the halogens. As such, 1% of a sample of gaseous iodine at atmospheric pressure is dissociated into iodine atoms at 575 °C. Temperatures greater than 750 °C are required for fluorine, chlorine, and bromine to dissociate to a similar extent. Most bonds to iodine are weaker than the analogous bonds to the lighter halogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup> Gaseous iodine is composed of I<sub>2</sub> molecules with an I–I bond length of 266.6 pm. The I–I bond is one of the longest single bonds known.  It is even longer (271.5 pm) in solid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthorhombic" title="Orthorhombic">orthorhombic</a> crystalline iodine, which has the same crystal structure as chlorine and bromine. (The record is held by iodine's neighbour <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>: the Xe–Xe bond length is 308.71 pm.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> As such, within the iodine molecule, significant electronic interactions occur with the two next-nearest neighbours of each atom, and these interactions give rise, in bulk iodine, to a shiny appearance and <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconducting</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup> Iodine is a two-dimensional semiconductor with a <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">band gap</a> of 1.3 eV (125 kJ/mol): it is a semiconductor in the plane of its crystalline layers and an insulator in the perpendicular direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_iodine" title="Isotopes of iodine">Isotopes of iodine</a></div>
<p>Of the thirty-seven known <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_iodine" title="Isotopes of iodine">isotopes of iodine</a>, only one occurs in nature, iodine-127.  The others are radioactive and have half-lives too short to be <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a>. As such, iodine is <a href="/wiki/Monoisotopic_element" title="Monoisotopic element">monoisotopic</a> and its atomic weight is known to great precision, as it is a constant of nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The longest-lived of the radioactive isotopes of iodine is <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">iodine-129</a>, which has a half-life of 15.7 million years, decaying via <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> to stable <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>-129.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-23">[23]</a></sup> Some iodine-129 was formed along with iodine-127 before the formation of the Solar System, but it has by now completely decayed away, making it an <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct radionuclide</a> that is nevertheless still useful in dating the history of the early Solar System or very old groundwaters, due to its mobility in the environment. Its former presence may be determined from an excess of its <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">daughter</a> xenon-129.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Traces of iodine-129 still exist today, as it is also a <a href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_nuclide" title="Cosmogenic nuclide">cosmogenic nuclide</a>, formed from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_spallation" title="Cosmic ray spallation">cosmic ray spallation</a> of atmospheric xenon: these traces make up 10<sup>−14</sup> to 10<sup>−10</sup> of all terrestrial iodine. It also occurs from open-air nuclear testing, and is not hazardous because of its incredibly long half-life, the longest of all fission products. At the peak of thermonuclear testing in the 1960s and 1970s, iodine-129 still made up only about 10<sup>−7</sup> of all terrestrial iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCOPE50_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCOPE50-29">[29]</a></sup> Excited states of iodine-127 and iodine-129 are often used in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_spectroscopy" title="Mössbauer spectroscopy">Mössbauer spectroscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other iodine radioisotopes have much shorter half-lives, no longer than days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-23">[23]</a></sup> Some of them have medical applications involving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_gland" title="Thyroid gland">thyroid gland</a>, where the iodine that enters the body is stored and concentrated. <a href="/wiki/Iodine-123" title="Iodine-123">Iodine-123</a> has a half-life of thirteen hours and decays by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tellurium-123" title="Tellurium-123">tellurium-123</a>, emitting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" title="Gamma radiation">gamma radiation</a>; it is used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a> imaging, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single_photon_emission_computed_tomography" title="Single photon emission computed tomography">single photon emission computed tomography</a> (SPECT) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" title="X-ray computed tomography">X-ray computed tomography</a> (X-Ray CT) scans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iodine-125" title="Iodine-125">Iodine-125</a> has a half-life of fifty-nine days, decaying by electron capture to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tellurium-125" title="Tellurium-125">tellurium-125</a> and emitting low-energy gamma radiation; the second-longest-lived iodine radioisotope, it has uses in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_assay" title="Biological assay">biological assays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a> imaging and in <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">radiation therapy</a> as <a href="/wiki/Brachytherapy" title="Brachytherapy">brachytherapy</a> to treat a number of conditions, including <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uveal_melanoma" title="Uveal melanoma">uveal melanomas</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_tumour" title="Brain tumour">brain tumours</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Finally, <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a>, with a half-life of eight days, beta decays to an excited state of stable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenon-131" title="Xenon-131">xenon-131</a> that then converts to the ground state by emitting gamma radiation. It is a common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission product</a> and thus is present in high levels in radioactive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fallout" title="Fallout">fallout</a>. It may then be absorbed through contaminated food, and will also accumulate in the thyroid. As it decays, it may cause damage to the thyroid. The primary risk from exposure to high levels of iodine-131 is the chance occurrence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic" title="Radiogenic">radiogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">thyroid cancer</a> in later life. Other risks include the possibility of non-cancerous growths and <a href="/wiki/Thyroiditis" title="Thyroiditis">thyroiditis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkees_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkees-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The usual means of protection against the negative effects of iodine-131 is by saturating the thyroid gland with stable iodine-127 in the form of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a> tablets, taken daily for optimal prophylaxis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> However, iodine-131 may also be used for medicinal purposes in <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">radiation therapy</a> for this very reason, when tissue destruction is desired after iodine uptake by the tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Iodine-131 is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_compounds">Chemistry and compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemistry and compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Though it is the least reactive of the halogens, iodine is still one of the more reactive elements. For example, while chlorine gas will halogenate <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (to <a href="/wiki/Phosgene" title="Phosgene">phosgene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrosyl_chloride" title="Nitrosyl chloride">nitrosyl chloride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfuryl_chloride" title="Sulfuryl chloride">sulfuryl chloride</a> respectively), iodine will not do so. Furthermore, iodination of metals tends to result in lower oxidation states than chlorination or bromination; for example, <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> metal reacts with chlorine to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhenium_hexachloride" title="Rhenium hexachloride">rhenium hexachloride</a>, but with bromine it forms only <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rhenium_pentabromide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rhenium pentabromide (page does not exist)">rhenium pentabromide</a> and iodine can achieve only <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rhenium_tetraiodide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rhenium tetraiodide (page does not exist)">rhenium tetraiodide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup> By the same token, however, since iodine has the lowest ionisation energy among the halogens and is the most easily oxidised of them, it has a more significant cationic chemistry and its higher oxidation states are rather more stable than those of bromine and chlorine, for example in <a href="/wiki/Iodine_heptafluoride" title="Iodine heptafluoride">iodine heptafluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood804_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood804-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_iodide">Hydrogen iodide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hydrogen iodide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The simplest compound of iodine is <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_iodide" title="Hydrogen iodide">hydrogen iodide</a>, HI. It is a colourless gas that reacts with oxygen to give water and iodine. Although it is useful in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iodination" title="Iodination">iodination</a> reactions in the laboratory, it does not have large-scale industrial uses, unlike the other hydrogen halides. Commercially, it is usually made by reacting iodine with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood809_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood809-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 I<sub>2</sub> + N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:0.8em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">H<sub>2</sub>O</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">⟶</span></span></span> 4 HI + N<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>At room temperature, it is a colourless gas, like all of the hydrogen halides except <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a>, since hydrogen cannot form strong <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> to the large and only mildly electronegative iodine atom. It melts at −51.0 °C and boils at −35.1 °C. It is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> compound that can exothermically dissociate at room temperature, although the process is very slow unless a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> is present: the reaction between hydrogen and iodine at room temperature to give hydrogen iodide does not proceed to completion. The H–I <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bond_dissociation_energy" title="Bond dissociation energy">bond dissociation energy</a> is likewise the smallest of the hydrogen halides, at 295 kJ/mol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood812_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood812-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aqueous hydrogen iodide is known as <a href="/wiki/Hydroiodic_acid" title="Hydroiodic acid">hydroiodic acid</a>, which is a strong acid. Hydrogen iodide is exceptionally soluble in water: one litre of water will dissolve 425 litres of hydrogen iodide, and the saturated solution has only four water molecules per molecule of hydrogen iodide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Commercial so-called "concentrated" hydroiodic acid usually contains 48–57% HI by mass; the solution forms an <a href="/wiki/Azeotrope" title="Azeotrope">azeotrope</a> with boiling point 126.7 °C at 56.7 g HI per 100 g solution. Hence hydroiodic acid cannot be concentrated past this point by evaporation of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood812_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood812-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a>, anhydrous liquid hydrogen iodide is difficult to work with as a solvent, because its boiling point is low, it has a small liquid range, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant">dielectric constant</a> is low and it does not dissociate appreciably into H<sub>2</sub>I<sup>+</sup> and <span class="chemf nowrap">HI<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ions – the latter, in any case, are much less stable than the <a href="/wiki/Bifluoride" title="Bifluoride">bifluoride</a> ions (<span class="chemf nowrap">HF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) due to the very weak hydrogen bonding between hydrogen and iodine, though its salts with very large and weakly polarising cations such as <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">Cs<sup>+</sup></a> and <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_ammonium_cation" title="Quaternary ammonium cation"><span class="chemf nowrap">NR<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a> (R = <a href="/wiki/Methyl_group" title="Methyl group">Me</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_group" title="Ethyl group">Et</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butyl_group" title="Butyl group">Bu<sup><i>n</i></sup></a>) may still be isolated. Anhydrous hydrogen iodide is a poor solvent, able to dissolve only small molecular compounds such as nitrosyl chloride and <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a>, or salts with very low <a href="/wiki/Lattice_energy" title="Lattice energy">lattice energies</a> such as tetraalkylammonium halides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood812_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood812-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_binary_iodides">Other binary iodides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other binary iodides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nearly all elements in the periodic table form binary iodides. The exceptions are decidedly in the minority and stem in each case from one of three causes: extreme inertness and reluctance to participate in chemical reactions (the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>); extreme nuclear instability hampering chemical investigation before decay and transmutation (many of the heaviest elements beyond <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>); and having an electronegativity higher than iodine's (<a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and the first three halogens), so that the resultant binary compounds are formally not iodides but rather oxides, nitrides, or halides of iodine. (Nonetheless, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_triiodide" title="Nitrogen triiodide">nitrogen triiodide</a> is named as an iodide as it is analogous to the other nitrogen trihalides.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood821_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood821-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Given the large size of the iodide anion and iodine's weak oxidising power, high oxidation states are difficult to achieve in binary iodides, the maximum known being in the pentaiodides of <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a>. Iodides can be made by reaction of an element or its oxide, hydroxide, or carbonate with hydroiodic acid, and then dehydrated by mildly high temperatures combined with either low pressure or anhydrous hydrogen iodide gas. These methods work best when the iodide product is stable to hydrolysis; otherwise, the possibilities include high-temperature oxidative iodination of the element with iodine or hydrogen iodide, high-temperature iodination of a metal oxide or other halide by iodine, a volatile metal halide, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetraiodide" title="Carbon tetraiodide">carbon tetraiodide</a>, or an organic iodide. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molybdenum(IV)_oxide" title="Molybdenum(IV) oxide">molybdenum(IV) oxide</a> reacts with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium(III)_iodide" title="Aluminium(III) iodide">aluminium(III) iodide</a> at 230 °C to give <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Molybdenum(II)_iodide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Molybdenum(II) iodide (page does not exist)">molybdenum(II) iodide</a>. An example involving halogen exchange is given below, involving the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Tantalum(V)_chloride" title="Tantalum(V) chloride">tantalum(V) chloride</a> with excess aluminium(III) iodide at 400 °C to give <a href="/wiki/Tantalum(V)_iodide" title="Tantalum(V) iodide">tantalum(V) iodide</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood821_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood821-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Lower iodides may be produced either through thermal decomposition or disproportionation, or by reducing the higher iodide with hydrogen or a metal, for example:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood821_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood821-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Most of the iodides of the pre-transition metals (groups 1, 2, and 3, along with the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> in the +2 and +3 oxidation states) are mostly ionic, while nonmetals tend to form covalent molecular iodides, as do metals in high oxidation states from +3 and above. Ionic iodides MI<sub><i>n</i></sub> tend to have the lowest melting and boiling points among the halides MX<sub><i>n</i></sub> of the same element, because the electrostatic forces of attraction between the cations and anions are weakest for the large iodide anion. In contrast, covalent iodides tend to instead have the highest melting and boiling points among the halides of the same element, since iodine is the most polarisable of the halogens and, having the most electrons among them, can contribute the most to van der Waals forces. Naturally, exceptions abound in intermediate iodides where one trend gives way to the other. Similarly, solubilities in water of predominantly ionic iodides (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>) are the greatest among ionic halides of that element, while those of covalent iodides (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>) are the lowest of that element. In particular, <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">silver iodide</a> is very insoluble in water and its formation is often used as a qualitative test for iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood821_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood821-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iodine_halides">Iodine halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Iodine halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The halogens form many binary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamagnetic" title="Diamagnetic">diamagnetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Interhalogen" title="Interhalogen">interhalogen</a> compounds with stoichiometries XY, XY<sub>3</sub>, XY<sub>5</sub>, and XY<sub>7</sub> (where X is heavier than Y), and iodine is no exception. Iodine forms all three possible diatomic interhalogens, a trifluoride and trichloride, as well as a pentafluoride and, exceptionally among the halogens, a heptafluoride. Numerous cationic and anionic derivatives are also characterised, such as the wine-red or bright orange compounds of <span class="chemf nowrap">ICl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and the dark brown or purplish black compounds of I<sub>2</sub>Cl<sup>+</sup>. Apart from these, some <a href="/wiki/Pseudohalogen" title="Pseudohalogen">pseudohalides</a> are also known, such as <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen_iodide" title="Cyanogen iodide">cyanogen iodide</a> (ICN), iodine <a href="/wiki/Thiocyanate" title="Thiocyanate">thiocyanate</a> (ISCN), and iodine <a href="/wiki/Azide" title="Azide">azide</a> (IN<sub>3</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood824_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood824-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iodine_monochloride1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="338" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Iodine_monochloride1.jpg/220px-Iodine_monochloride1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Iodine_monochloride1.jpg/330px-Iodine_monochloride1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/Iodine_monochloride1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iodine_monochloride1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iodine monochloride</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iodine_monofluoride" title="Iodine monofluoride">Iodine monofluoride</a> (IF) is unstable at room temperature and disproportionates very readily and irreversibly to iodine and <a href="/wiki/Iodine_pentafluoride" title="Iodine pentafluoride">iodine pentafluoride</a>, and thus cannot be obtained pure. It can be synthesised from the reaction of iodine with fluorine gas in <a href="/wiki/Trichlorofluoromethane" title="Trichlorofluoromethane">trichlorofluoromethane</a> at −45 °C, with <a href="/wiki/Iodine_trifluoride" title="Iodine trifluoride">iodine trifluoride</a> in trichlorofluoromethane at −78 °C, or with <a href="/wiki/Silver(I)_fluoride" title="Silver(I) fluoride">silver(I) fluoride</a> at 0 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood824_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood824-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iodine_monochloride" title="Iodine monochloride">Iodine monochloride</a> (ICl) and <a href="/wiki/Iodine_monobromide" title="Iodine monobromide">iodine monobromide</a> (IBr), on the other hand, are moderately stable. The former, a volatile red-brown compound, was discovered independently by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> in 1813–4 not long after the discoveries of chlorine and iodine, and it mimics the intermediate halogen bromine so well that <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a> was misled into mistaking bromine (which he had found) for iodine monochloride. Iodine monochloride and iodine monobromide may be prepared simply by reacting iodine with chlorine or bromine at room temperature and purified by <a href="/wiki/Fractional_crystallization_(chemistry)" title="Fractional crystallization (chemistry)">fractional crystallisation</a>. Both are quite reactive and attack even <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, though not <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>. Their reaction with organic compounds depends on conditions. Iodine chloride vapour tends to chlorinate <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salicyclic_acid" title="Salicyclic acid">salicyclic acid</a>, since when iodine chloride undergoes homolytic dissociation, chlorine and iodine are produced and the former is more reactive. However, iodine chloride in tetrachloromethane solution results in iodination being the main reaction, since now heterolytic fission of the I–Cl bond occurs and I<sup>+</sup> attacks phenol as an electrophile. However, iodine monobromide tends to brominate phenol even in tetrachloromethane solution because it tends to dissociate into its elements in solution, and bromine is more reactive than iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood824_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood824-43">[43]</a></sup> When liquid, iodine monochloride and iodine monobromide dissociate into <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>X<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">IX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> anions (X = Cl, Br); thus they are significant conductors of electricity and can be used as ionising solvents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood824_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood824-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iodine_trifluoride" title="Iodine trifluoride">Iodine trifluoride</a> (IF<sub>3</sub>) is an unstable yellow solid that decomposes above −28 °C. It is thus little-known. It is difficult to produce because fluorine gas would tend to oxidise iodine all the way to the pentafluoride; reaction at low temperature with <a href="/wiki/Xenon_difluoride" title="Xenon difluoride">xenon difluoride</a> is necessary. <a href="/wiki/Iodine_trichloride" title="Iodine trichloride">Iodine trichloride</a>, which exists in the solid state as the planar dimer I<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub>, is a bright yellow solid, synthesised by reacting iodine with liquid chlorine at −80 °C; caution is necessary during purification because it easily dissociates to iodine monochloride and chlorine and hence can act as a strong chlorinating agent. Liquid iodine trichloride conducts electricity, possibly indicating dissociation to <span class="chemf nowrap">ICl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">ICl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood828_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood828-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iodine_pentafluoride" title="Iodine pentafluoride">Iodine pentafluoride</a> (IF<sub>5</sub>), a colourless, volatile liquid, is the most thermodynamically stable iodine fluoride, and can be made by reacting iodine with fluorine gas at room temperature. It is a fluorinating agent, but is mild enough to store in glass apparatus. Again, slight electrical conductivity is present in the liquid state because of dissociation to <span class="chemf nowrap">IF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">IF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>. The <a href="/wiki/Pentagonal_bipyramidal_molecular_geometry" title="Pentagonal bipyramidal molecular geometry">pentagonal bipyramidal</a> <a href="/wiki/Iodine_heptafluoride" title="Iodine heptafluoride">iodine heptafluoride</a> (IF<sub>7</sub>) is an extremely powerful fluorinating agent, behind only <a href="/wiki/Chlorine_trifluoride" title="Chlorine trifluoride">chlorine trifluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine_pentafluoride" title="Chlorine pentafluoride">chlorine pentafluoride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bromine_pentafluoride" title="Bromine pentafluoride">bromine pentafluoride</a> among the interhalogens: it reacts with almost all the elements even at low temperatures, fluorinates <a href="/wiki/Pyrex" title="Pyrex">Pyrex</a> glass to form iodine(VII) oxyfluoride (IOF<sub>5</sub>), and sets carbon monoxide on fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood832_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood832-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iodine_oxides_and_oxoacids">Iodine oxides and oxoacids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Iodine oxides and oxoacids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="1100" height="77" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png/150px-Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png/225px-Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png/300px-Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iodine-pentoxide-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of iodine pentoxide</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iodine_oxide" title="Iodine oxide">Iodine oxides</a> are the most stable of all the halogen oxides, because of the strong I–O bonds resulting from the large electronegativity difference between iodine and oxygen, and they have been known for the longest time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-King-20">[20]</a></sup> The stable, white, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Iodine_pentoxide" title="Iodine pentoxide">iodine pentoxide</a> (I<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) has been known since its formation in 1813 by Gay-Lussac and Davy. It is most easily made by the dehydration of <a href="/wiki/Iodic_acid" title="Iodic acid">iodic acid</a> (HIO<sub>3</sub>), of which it is the anhydride. It will quickly oxidise carbon monoxide completely to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> at room temperature, and is thus a useful reagent in determining carbon monoxide concentration. It also oxidises nitrogen oxide, <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>. It reacts with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">sulfur trioxide</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peroxydisulfuryl_difluoride&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peroxydisulfuryl difluoride (page does not exist)">peroxydisulfuryl difluoride</a> (S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F<sub>2</sub>) to form salts of the iodyl cation, [IO<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup>, and is reduced by concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acids</a> to iodosyl salts involving [IO]<sup>+</sup>. It may be fluorinated by fluorine, bromine trifluoride, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_tetrafluoride" title="Sulfur tetrafluoride">sulfur tetrafluoride</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Chloryl_fluoride" title="Chloryl fluoride">chloryl fluoride</a>, resulting iodine pentafluoride, which also reacts with iodine pentoxide, giving iodine(V) oxyfluoride, IOF<sub>3</sub>. A few other less stable oxides are known, notably I<sub>4</sub>O<sub>9</sub> and I<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>; their structures have not been determined, but reasonable guesses are I<sup>III</sup>(I<sup>V</sup>O<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and [IO]<sup>+</sup>[IO<sub>3</sub>]<sup>−</sup> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood851_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood851-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-top:0; margin-left:1em; text-align:center; font-size:10pt; line-height:11pt; width:25%;">
