</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>More important are the four oxoacids: <a href="/wiki/Hypoiodous_acid" title="Hypoiodous acid">hypoiodous acid</a> (HIO), <a href="/wiki/Iodous_acid" title="Iodous acid">iodous acid</a> (HIO<sub>2</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Iodic_acid" title="Iodic acid">iodic acid</a> (HIO<sub>3</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Periodic_acid" title="Periodic acid">periodic acid</a> (HIO<sub>4</sub> or H<sub>5</sub>IO<sub>6</sub>). When iodine dissolves in aqueous solution, the following reactions occur:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood853_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood853-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><table>
<tbody><tr>
<td>I<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O</td>
<td>⇌ HIO + H<sup>+</sup> + I<sup>−</sup></td>
<td><i>K</i><sub>ac</sub> = 2.0 × 10<sup>−13</sup> mol<sup>2</sup> l<sup>−2</sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>I<sub>2</sub> + 2 OH<sup>−</sup></td>
<td>⇌ IO<sup>−</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O + I<sup>−</sup></td>
<td><i>K</i><sub>alk</sub> = 30 mol<sup>−1</sup> l
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<p>Hypoiodous acid is unstable to disproportionation. The hypoiodite ions thus formed disproportionate immediately to give iodide and iodate:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood853_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood853-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><table>
<tbody><tr>
<td>3 IO<sup>−</sup> ⇌ 2 I<sup>−</sup> + <span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></td>
<td><i>K</i> = 10<sup>20</sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<p>Iodous acid and iodite are even less stable and exist only as a fleeting intermediate in the oxidation of iodide to iodate, if at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood853_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood853-47">[47]</a></sup> Iodates are by far the most important of these compounds, which can be made by oxidising <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a> iodides with oxygen at 600 °C and high pressure, or by oxidising iodine with <a href="/wiki/Chlorate" title="Chlorate">chlorates</a>. Unlike chlorates, which disproportionate very slowly to form chloride and perchlorate, iodates are stable to disproportionation in both acidic and alkaline solutions. From these, salts of most metals can be obtained. Iodic acid is most easily made by oxidation of an aqueous iodine suspension by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> or fuming <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>. Iodate has the weakest oxidising power of the halates, but reacts the quickest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood863_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood863-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many periodates are known, including not only the expected tetrahedral <span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, but also square-pyramidal <span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, octahedral orthoperiodate <span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, [IO<sub>3</sub>(OH)<sub>3</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>, [I<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub>(OH<sub>2</sub>)]<sup>4−</sup>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span></span>. They are usually made by oxidising alkaline <a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodate" title="Sodium iodate">sodium iodate</a> electrochemically (with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead(IV)_oxide" title="Lead(IV) oxide">lead(IV) oxide</a> as the anode) or by chlorine gas:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood872_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood872-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 6 OH<sup>−</sup> → <span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O + 2 e<sup>−</sup></dd>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 6 OH<sup>−</sup> + Cl<sub>2</sub> → <span class="chemf nowrap">IO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> + 2 Cl<sup>−</sup> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>They are thermodymically and kinetically powerful oxidising agents, quickly oxidising Mn<sup>2+</sup> to <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate"><span class="chemf nowrap">MnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>, and cleaving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycol" title="Glycol">glycols</a>, α-<a href="/wiki/Diketone" title="Diketone">diketones</a>, α-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ketol" title="Ketol">ketols</a>, α-<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aminoalcohols&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aminoalcohols (page does not exist)">aminoalcohols</a>, and α-<a href="/wiki/Diamine" title="Diamine">diamines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood872_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood872-49">[49]</a></sup> Orthoperiodate especially stabilises high oxidation states among metals because of its very high negative charge of −5. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthoperiodic_acid" title="Orthoperiodic acid">Orthoperiodic acid</a>, H<sub>5</sub>IO<sub>6</sub>, is stable, and dehydrates at 100 °C in a vacuum to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaperiodic_acid" title="Metaperiodic acid">metaperiodic acid</a>, HIO<sub>4</sub>. Attempting to go further does not result in the nonexistent iodine heptoxide (I<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), but rather iodine pentoxide and oxygen. Periodic acid may be protonated by <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> to give the <span class="chemf nowrap">I(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> cation, isoelectronic to Te(OH)<sub>6</sub> and <span class="chemf nowrap">Sb(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, and giving salts with bisulfate and sulfate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-King-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polyiodine_compounds">Polyiodine compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Polyiodine compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When iodine dissolves in strong acids, such as fuming sulfuric acid, a bright blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" title="Paramagnetic">paramagnetic</a> solution including <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> cations is formed. A solid salt of the diiodine cation may be obtained by oxidising iodine with <a href="/wiki/Antimony_pentafluoride" title="Antimony pentafluoride">antimony pentafluoride</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-King-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 I<sub>2</sub> + 5 SbF<sub>5</sub> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">SO<sub>2</sub></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="font-size: 120%;">⟶</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">20°C</span></span></span> 2 I<sub>2</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>F<sub>11</sub> + SbF<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The salt I<sub>2</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>F<sub>11</sub> is dark blue, and the blue <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> analogue I<sub>2</sub>Ta<sub>2</sub>F<sub>11</sub> is also known. Whereas the I–I bond length in I<sub>2</sub> is 267 pm, that in <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is only 256 pm as the missing electron in the latter has been removed from an antibonding orbital, making the bond stronger and hence shorter. In <a href="/wiki/Fluorosulfuric_acid" title="Fluorosulfuric acid">fluorosulfuric acid</a> solution, deep-blue <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> reversibly dimerises below −60 °C, forming red rectangular diamagnetic <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>. Other polyiodine cations are not as well-characterised, including bent dark-brown or black <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> and centrosymmetric <i>C</i><sub>2<i>h</i></sub> green or black <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, known in the <span class="chemf nowrap">AsF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">AlCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> salts among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-King-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood842_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood842-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The only important polyiodide anion in aqueous solution is linear <a href="/wiki/Triiodide" title="Triiodide">triiodide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>. Its formation explains why the solubility of iodine in water may be increased by the addition of potassium iodide solution:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-King-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>I<sub>2</sub> + I<sup>−</sup> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> (<i>K</i><sub>eq</sub> = ~700 at 20 °C)</dd></dl>
<p>Many other polyiodides may be found when solutions containing iodine and iodide crystallise, such as <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>, whose salts with large, weakly polarising cations such as <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">Cs<sup>+</sup></a> may be isolated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-King-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood835_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood835-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organoiodine_compounds">Organoiodine compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Organoiodine compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organoiodine_compound" title="Organoiodine compound">Organoiodine compound</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IBXAcid.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="506" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/IBXAcid.png/220px-IBXAcid.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/IBXAcid.png/330px-IBXAcid.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/IBXAcid.png/440px-IBXAcid.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IBXAcid.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the oxidising agent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2-iodoxybenzoic_acid" title="2-iodoxybenzoic acid">2-iodoxybenzoic acid</a></div></div></div>
<p>Organoiodine compounds have been fundamental in the development of organic synthesis, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Hofmann_elimination" title="Hofmann elimination">Hofmann elimination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Williamson_ether_synthesis" title="Williamson ether synthesis">Williamson ether synthesis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Wurtz_reaction" title="Wurtz reaction">Wurtz coupling reaction</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard reagents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>–iodine bond is a common functional group that forms part of core <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>; formally, these compounds may be thought of as organic derivatives of the iodide anion. The simplest organoiodine compounds, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_iodide" title="Alkyl iodide">alkyl iodides</a>, may be synthesised by the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a> with <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_triiodide" title="Phosphorus triiodide">phosphorus triiodide</a>; these may then be used in <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_substitution" title="Nucleophilic substitution">nucleophilic substitution</a> reactions, or for preparing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard reagents</a>. The C–I bond is the weakest of all the carbon–halogen bonds due to the minuscule difference in electronegativity between carbon (2.55) and iodine (2.66). As such, iodide is the best <a href="/wiki/Leaving_group" title="Leaving group">leaving group</a> among the halogens, to such an extent that many organoiodine compounds turn yellow when stored over time due to decomposition into elemental iodine; as such, they are commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>, because of the easy formation and cleavage of the C–I bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> They are also significantly denser than the other organohalogen compounds thanks to the high atomic weight of iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blanksby_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-blanksby-57">[57]</a></sup> A few organic oxidising agents like the <a href="/wiki/Iodane" title="Iodane">iodanes</a> contain iodine in a higher oxidation state than −1, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2-iodoxybenzoic_acid" title="2-iodoxybenzoic acid">2-iodoxybenzoic acid</a>, a common reagent for the oxidation of alcohols to <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Iodobenzene_dichloride" title="Iodobenzene dichloride">iodobenzene dichloride</a> (PhICl<sub>2</sub>), used for the selective chlorination of <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkyne" title="Alkyne">alkynes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> One of the more well-known uses of organoiodine compounds is the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iodoform_test" title="Iodoform test">iodoform test</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Iodoform" title="Iodoform">iodoform</a> (CHI<sub>3</sub>) is produced by the exhaustive iodination of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methyl_ketone" title="Methyl ketone">methyl ketone</a> (or another compound capable of being oxidised to a methyl ketone), as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-March_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-March-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iodoform_synthesis.svg"><img alt="Iodoform synthesis.svg" data-file-height="285" data-file-width="620" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Iodoform_synthesis.svg/450px-Iodoform_synthesis.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Iodoform_synthesis.svg/675px-Iodoform_synthesis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Iodoform_synthesis.svg/900px-Iodoform_synthesis.svg.png 2x" width="450"/></a></dd></dl>
<p>Some drawbacks of using organoiodine compounds as compared to organochlorine or organobromine compounds is the greater expense and toxicity of the iodine derivatives, since iodine is expensive and organoiodine compounds are stronger alkylating agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Iodoacetamide" title="Iodoacetamide">iodoacetamide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodoacetic_acid" title="Iodoacetic acid">iodoacetic acid</a> denature proteins by irreversibly alkylating <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> residues and preventing the reformation of <a href="/wiki/Disulfide" title="Disulfide">disulfide</a> linkages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Halogen exchange to produce iodoalkanes by the <a href="/wiki/Finkelstein_reaction" title="Finkelstein reaction">Finkelstein reaction</a> is slightly complicated by the fact that iodide is a better leaving group than chloride or bromide. The difference is nevertheless small enough that the reaction can be driven to completion by exploiting the differential solubility of halide salts, or by using a large excess of the halide salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-March_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-March-60">[60]</a></sup> In the classic Finkelstein reaction, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_chloride" title="Alkyl chloride">alkyl chloride</a> or an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_bromide" title="Alkyl bromide">alkyl bromide</a> is converted to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_iodide" title="Alkyl iodide">alkyl iodide</a> by treatment with a solution of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodide" title="Sodium iodide">sodium iodide</a> in <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>.  Sodium iodide is soluble in acetone and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bromide" title="Sodium bromide">sodium bromide</a> are not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The reaction is driven toward products by <a href="/wiki/Law_of_mass_action" title="Law of mass action">mass action</a> due to the precipitation of the insoluble salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Occurrence and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Iodine is the least abundant of the stable halogens, comprising only 0.46 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> of Earth's crustal rocks (compare: fluorine 544 ppm, chlorine 126 ppm, bromine 2.5 ppm). Among the eighty-four elements which occur in significant quantities (elements 1–42, 44–60, 62–83, and 90–92), it ranks sixty-first in abundance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood795_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood795-66">[66]</a></sup> Iodide minerals are rare, and most deposits that are concentrated enough for economical extraction are <a href="/wiki/Iodate" title="Iodate">iodate</a> minerals instead. Examples include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lautarite" title="Lautarite">lautarite</a>, Ca(IO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dietzeite&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dietzeite (page does not exist)">dietzeite</a>, 7Ca(IO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·8CaCrO<sub>4</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood795_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood795-66">[66]</a></sup> These are the minerals that occur as trace impurities in the <a href="/wiki/Caliche" title="Caliche">caliche</a>, found in Chile, whose main product is <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">sodium nitrate</a>. In total, they can contain at least 0.02% and at most 1% iodine by weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elzea_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elzea-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodate" title="Sodium iodate">Sodium iodate</a> is extracted from the caliche and reduced to iodide by <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bisulfite" title="Sodium bisulfite">sodium bisulfite</a>. This solution is then reacted with freshly extracted iodate, resulting in comproportionation to iodine, which may be filtered off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-10"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The caliche was the main source of iodine in the 19th century and continues to be important today, replacing <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a> (which is no longer an economically viable source),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> but in the late 20th century <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brines</a> emerged as a comparable source. The Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minami_Kanto_gas_field" title="Minami Kanto gas field">Minami Kanto gas field</a> east of <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> and the American <a href="/wiki/Anadarko_Basin" title="Anadarko Basin">Anadarko Basin</a> gas field in northwest <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a> are the two largest such sources. The brine is hotter than 60 °C from the depth of the source. The <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> is first <a href="/wiki/List_of_purification_methods_in_chemistry" title="List of purification methods in chemistry">purified</a> and acidified using <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, then the iodide present is oxidised to iodine with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>. An iodine solution is produced, but is dilute and must be concentrated. Air is blown into the solution to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaporate" title="Evaporate">evaporate</a> the iodine, which is passed into an absorbing tower where <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> reduces the iodine. The <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_iodide" title="Hydrogen iodide">hydrogen iodide</a> (HI) is reacted with chlorine to precipitate the iodine. After filtering and purification the iodine is packed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elzea_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elzea-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 HI + Cl<sub>2</sub> → I<sub>2</sub>↑ + 2 HCl</dd>
<dd>I<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O + SO<sub>2</sub> → 2 HI + H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub></dd>
<dd>2 HI + Cl<sub>2</sub> → I<sub>2</sub>↓ + 2 HCl</dd></dl>
<p>These sources ensure that Chile and Japan are the largest producers of iodine today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood795_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood795-66">[66]</a></sup> Alternatively, the brine may be treated with <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a> to precipitate out iodine as <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">silver iodide</a>, which is then decomposed by reaction with iron to form metallic silver and a solution of <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_iodide" title="Iron(II) iodide">iron(II) iodide</a>. The iodine may then be liberated by displacement with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood799_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood799-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Unlike chlorine and bromine, which have one significant main use dwarfing all others, iodine is used in many applications of varying importance. About half of all produced iodine goes into various organoiodine compounds; another 15% remains as the pure element, another 15% is used to form <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a>, and another 15% for other inorganic iodine compounds. The remaining 5% is for minor uses. Among the major uses of iodine compounds are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a>, animal feed supplements, stabilisers, dyes, colourants and pigments, pharmaceutical, sanitation (from <a href="/wiki/Tincture_of_iodine" title="Tincture of iodine">tincture of iodine</a>), and photography; minor uses include smog inhibition, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_seeding" title="Cloud seeding">cloud seeding</a>, and various uses in analytical chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-11"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="860" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg/220px-Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg/330px-Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg/440px-Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Testing_seed_for_starch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Testing a seed for starch with a solution of iodine</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Potassium_tetraiodomercurate(II)" title="Potassium tetraiodomercurate(II)">Potassium tetraiodomercurate(II)</a>, K<sub>2</sub>HgI<sub>4</sub>, is also known as Nessler's reagent. It is often used as a sensitive spot test for <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. Similarly, Cu<sub>2</sub>HgI<sub>4</sub> is used as a precipitating reagent to test for <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloids</a>. The iodide and iodate anions are often used for quantitative volumetric analysis, for example in <a href="/wiki/Iodometry" title="Iodometry">iodometry</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iodine_clock_reaction" title="Iodine clock reaction">iodine clock reaction</a> (in which iodine also serves as a test for starch, forming a dark blue complex),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood800_16-12"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood800-16">[16]</a></sup> and aqueous alkaline iodine solution is used in the iodoform test for methyl ketones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-March_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-March-60">[60]</a></sup> The iodine test for starch is still used to detect <a href="/wiki/Counterfeit" title="Counterfeit">counterfeit</a> banknotes printed on starch-containing paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elemental_iodine">Elemental iodine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Elemental iodine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iodine_(medical_use)" title="Iodine (medical use)">Iodine (medical use)</a></div>
<p>Elemental iodine is used as a disinfectant either as the element, or as the water-soluble <a href="/wiki/Triiodide" title="Triiodide">triiodide</a> anion I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> generated <i>in situ</i> by adding <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> to poorly water-soluble elemental iodine (the reverse chemical reaction makes some free elemental iodine available for antisepsis). Elemental iodine may also be used to treat <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">iodine deficiency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2008_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2008-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the alternative, iodine may be produced from <a href="/wiki/Iodophor" title="Iodophor">iodophors</a>, which contain iodine complexed with a solubilizing agent (iodide ion may be thought of loosely as the iodophor in triiodide water solutions). Examples of such preparations include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tincture_of_iodine" title="Tincture of iodine">Tincture of iodine</a>: iodine in ethanol, or iodine and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodide" title="Sodium iodide">sodium iodide</a> in a mixture of ethanol and water.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lugol%27s_iodine" title="Lugol's iodine">Lugol's iodine</a>: iodine and iodide in water alone, forming mostly triiodide. Unlike tincture of iodine, Lugol's iodine has a minimised amount of the free iodine (I<sub>2</sub>) component.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Povidone_iodine" title="Povidone iodine">Povidone iodine</a> (an <a href="/wiki/Iodophor" title="Iodophor">iodophor</a>).</li></ul>
<p>The antimicrobial action of iodine is quick and works at low concentrations, and thus it is used in operating theatres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Its specific mode of action is unknown. It penetrates into microorganisms and attacks particular amino acids (such as <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleotides" title="Nucleotides">nucleotides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, ultimately resulting in <a href="/wiki/Cell_death" title="Cell death">cell death</a>. It also has an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiviral" title="Antiviral">antiviral</a> action, but nonlipid viruses and <a href="/wiki/Parvovirus" title="Parvovirus">parvoviruses</a> are less sensitive than lipid enveloped viruses. Iodine probably attacks surface proteins of <a href="/wiki/Viral_envelope" title="Viral envelope">enveloped</a> viruses, and it may also destabilise membrane fatty acids by reacting with <a href="/wiki/Saturated_and_unsaturated_compounds" title="Saturated and unsaturated compounds">unsaturated</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93carbon_bond" title="Carbon–carbon bond">carbon bonds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9880479_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9880479-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_formulations">Other formulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other formulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In medicine, a saturated solution of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a> is used to treat acute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyrotoxicosis" title="Thyrotoxicosis">thyrotoxicosis</a>. It is also used to block uptake of <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a> in the thyroid gland (see isotopes section above), when this isotope is used as part of radiopharmaceuticals (such as <a href="/wiki/Iobenguane" title="Iobenguane">iobenguane</a>) that are not targeted to the thyroid or thyroid-type tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iodine-131 (usually as iodide) is a component of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a>, and is particularly dangerous owing to the thyroid gland's propensity to concentrate ingested iodine and retain it for periods longer than this isotope's radiological half-life of eight days. For this reason, people at risk of exposure to environmental radioactive iodine (iodine-131) in fallout may be instructed to take non-radioactive potassium iodide tablets. The typical adult dose is one 130 mg tablet per 24 hours, supplying 100 mg (100,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrograms" title="Micrograms">micrograms</a>) of ionic iodine. (The typical daily dose of iodine for normal health is of order 100 micrograms; see "Dietary Intake" below.) Ingestion of this large dose of non-radioactive iodine minimises the uptake of radioactive iodine by the thyroid gland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diatrizoic_acid.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="750" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Diatrizoic_acid.svg/220px-Diatrizoic_acid.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Diatrizoic_acid.svg/330px-Diatrizoic_acid.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Diatrizoic_acid.svg/440px-Diatrizoic_acid.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diatrizoic_acid.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatrizoic_acid" title="Diatrizoic acid">Diatrizoic acid</a>, an iodine-containing radiocontrast agent</div></div></div>
<p>As an element with high <a href="/wiki/Electron_density" title="Electron density">electron density</a> and atomic number, iodine absorbs X-rays weaker than 33.3 keV due to the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a> of the innermost electrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Organoiodine compounds are used with intravenous injection as X-ray <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiocontrast" title="Radiocontrast">radiocontrast</a> agents. This application is often in conjunction with advanced X-ray techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Angiography" title="Angiography">angiography</a> and <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scanning</a>. At present, all water-soluble radiocontrast agents rely on iodine.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The production of <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine_dihydroiodide" title="Ethylenediamine dihydroiodide">ethylenediamine dihydroiodide</a>, provided as a nutritional supplement for livestock, consumes a large portion of available iodine. Another significant use is a catalyst for the production of <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Monsanto_process" title="Monsanto process">Monsanto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cativa_process" title="Cativa process">Cativa processes</a>. In these technologies, which support the world's demand for acetic acid, <a href="/wiki/Hydroiodic_acid" title="Hydroiodic acid">hydroiodic acid</a> converts the <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> feedstock into methyl iodide, which undergoes <a href="/wiki/Carbonylation" title="Carbonylation">carbonylation</a>. Hydrolysis of the resulting acetyl iodide regenerates hydroiodic acid and gives acetic acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inorganic iodides find specialised uses. Titanium, zirconium, hafnium, and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> are purified by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Arkel_process" title="Van Arkel process">van Arkel process</a>, which involves the reversible formation of the tetraiodides of these elements. Silver iodide is a major ingredient to traditional photographic film. Thousands of kilograms of silver iodide are used annually for <a href="/wiki/Cloud_seeding" title="Cloud seeding">cloud seeding</a> to induce rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>The organoiodine compound <a href="/wiki/Erythrosine" title="Erythrosine">erythrosine</a> is an important food coloring agent. Perfluoroalkyl iodides are precursors to important surfactants, such as <a href="/wiki/Perfluorooctanesulfonic_acid" title="Perfluorooctanesulfonic acid">perfluorooctanesulfonic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iodine_in_biology" title="Iodine in biology">Iodine in biology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thyroid_system.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1010" data-file-width="871" height="359" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thyroid_system.svg/310px-Thyroid_system.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thyroid_system.svg/465px-Thyroid_system.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thyroid_system.svg/620px-Thyroid_system.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thyroid_system.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> system of the thyroid hormones <a href="/wiki/Triiodothyronine" title="Triiodothyronine">T<sub>3</sub></a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroxine" title="Thyroxine">T<sub>4</sub></a></div></div></div>
<p>Iodine is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_element" title="Essential element">essential element</a> for life and, at atomic number <i>Z</i> = 53, is the heaviest element commonly needed by living organisms. (<a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">Lanthanum</a> and the other <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> with <i>Z</i> = 74, are used by a few microorganisms.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> It is required for the synthesis of the growth-regulating thyroid hormones <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroxine" title="Thyroxine">thyroxine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triiodothyronine" title="Triiodothyronine">triiodothyronine</a> (T<sub>4</sub> and T<sub>3</sub> respectively, named after their number of iodine atoms). A deficiency of iodine leads to decreased production of T<sub>3</sub> and T<sub>4</sub> and a concomitant enlargement of the <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid tissue</a> in an attempt to obtain more iodine, causing the disease known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_goitre" title="Simple goitre">simple goitre</a>. The major form of thyroid hormone in the blood is thyroxine (T<sub>4</sub>), which has a longer half-life than T<sub>3</sub>. In humans, the ratio of T<sub>4</sub> to T<sub>3</sub> released into the blood is between 14:1 and 20:1. T<sub>4</sub> is converted to the active T<sub>3</sub> (three to four times more potent than T<sub>4</sub>) within <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> by <a href="/wiki/Deiodinase" title="Deiodinase">deiodinases</a> (5'-iodinase). These are further processed by <a href="/wiki/Decarboxylation" title="Decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a> and deiodination to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iodothyronamine" title="Iodothyronamine">iodothyronamine</a> (T<sub>1</sub>a) and <a href="/wiki/Thyronamine" title="Thyronamine">thyronamine</a> (T<sub>0</sub>a'). All three isoforms of the deiodinases are <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>-containing enzymes; thus dietary selenium is essential for T<sub>3</sub> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iodine accounts for 65% of the molecular weight of T<sub>4</sub> and 59% of T<sub>3</sub>. Fifteen to 20 mg of iodine is concentrated in thyroid tissue and hormones, but 70% of all iodine in the body is found in other tissues, including mammary glands, eyes, gastric mucosa, fetal thymus, cerebro-spinal fluid and choroid plexus, arterial walls, the cervix, and salivary glands. In the cells of those tissues, iodide enters directly by <a href="/wiki/Sodium-iodide_symporter" title="Sodium-iodide symporter">sodium-iodide symporter</a> (NIS). The action of iodine in mammary tissue is related to fetal and neonatal development, but in the other tissues, it is (at least) partially unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick2008_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick2008-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_intake">Dietary intake</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Dietary intake">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recommendations by the United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" title="Institute of Medicine">Institute of Medicine</a> are between 110 and 130 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">µg</a> for infants up to 12 months, 90 µg for children up to eight years, 130 µg for children up to 13 years, 150 µg for adults, 220 µg for pregnant women and 290 µg for lactation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL) for adults is 1,100 μg/day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InstituteofMedicine_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-InstituteofMedicine-86">[86]</a></sup> This upper limit was assessed by analyzing the effect of supplementation on <a href="/wiki/Thyroid-stimulating_hormone" title="Thyroid-stimulating hormone">thyroid-stimulating hormone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick2008_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick2008-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The thyroid gland needs no more than 70 μg/day to synthesise the requisite daily amounts of T4 and T3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-4">[4]</a></sup> The higher recommended daily allowance levels of iodine seem necessary for optimal function of a number of body systems, including <a href="/wiki/Lactation" title="Lactation">lactating breast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastric_mucosa" title="Gastric mucosa">gastric mucosa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salivary_gland" title="Salivary gland">salivary glands</a>, brain cells, <a href="/wiki/Choroid_plexus" title="Choroid plexus">choroid plexus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arterial walls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural sources of dietary iodine include <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, such as fish, seaweeds (such as <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>, dairy products and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eggs_(food)" title="Eggs (food)">eggs</a> so long as the animals received enough iodine, and plants grown on iodine-rich soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medlineplus002421_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-medlineplus002421-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iodised_salt" title="Iodised salt">Iodised salt</a> is fortified with iodine in the form of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodide" title="Sodium iodide">sodium iodide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medlineplus002421_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-medlineplus002421-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_Thyroid_Association_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-American_Thyroid_Association-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2000, the median intake of iodine from food in the United States was 240 to 300 μg/day for men and 190 to 210 μg/day for women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InstituteofMedicine_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-InstituteofMedicine-86">[86]</a></sup> The general US population has adequate iodine nutrition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lueng_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lueng-94">[94]</a></sup> with women of childbearing age and pregnant women having a possible mild risk of deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lueng_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lueng-94">[94]</a></sup> In Japan, consumption was considered much higher, ranging between 5,280 μg/day to 13,800 μg/day from dietary seaweed or <a href="/wiki/Kombu" title="Kombu">kombu</a> kelp,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick2008_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick2008-84">[84]</a></sup> often in the form of Kombu <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">Umami</a> extracts for soup stock and potato chips. However, new studies suggest that Japan's consumption is closer to 1,000–3,000 μg/day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The adult UL in Japan was last revised to 3,000 µg/day in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>After iodine fortification programs such as iodisation of salt have been implemented, some cases of iodine-induced <a href="/wiki/Hyperthyroidism" title="Hyperthyroidism">hyperthyroidism</a> have been observed (so-called <a href="/wiki/Jod-Basedow_phenomenon" title="Jod-Basedow phenomenon">Jod-Basedow phenomenon</a>). The condition seems to occur mainly in people over forty, and the risk appears higher when iodine deficiency is severe and the initial rise in iodine intake is high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deficiency">Deficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Deficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">Iodine deficiency</a></div>
<p>In areas where there is little iodine in the diet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dissanayake_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dissanayake-98">[98]</a></sup> typically remote inland areas and semi-arid equatorial climates where no marine foods are eaten, <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">iodine deficiency</a> gives rise to hypothyroidism, symptoms of which are extreme fatigue, <a href="/wiki/Goitre" title="Goitre">goitre</a>, mental slowing, depression, weight gain, and low basal body temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Iodine deficiency is the leading cause of preventable <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_disability" title="Intellectual disability">intellectual disability</a>, a result that occurs primarily when babies or small children are rendered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothyroid" title="Hypothyroid">hypothyroidic</a> by a lack of the element. The addition of iodine to table salt has largely eliminated this problem in the wealthier nations, but iodine deficiency remains a serious public health problem today in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Iodine deficiency is also a problem in certain areas of Europe. Information processing, fine motor skills, and visual problem solving are improved by iodine repletion in moderately iodine-deficient children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GHS_hazard_statement" title="GHS hazard statement">GHS hazard statements</a></div>
</td>
<td><abbr class="abbr" title="Harmful in contact with skin">H312</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Harmful if inhaled">H332</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Causes skin irritation">H315</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Causes serious eye irritation">H319</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="May cause respiratory irritation">H335</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Causes damage to organs through prolonged or repeated exposure">H372</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Very toxic to aquatic life">H400</abbr>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/GHS_precautionary_statements" title="GHS precautionary statements">GHS precautionary statements</a></div>
</td>
<td><abbr class="abbr" title="Avoid breathing dust/fume/gas/mist/vapours/spray">P261</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Avoid release to the environment">P273</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Wear protective gloves/protective clothing/eye protection/face protection">P280</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="IF IN EYES:">P305</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Rinse cautiously with water for several minutes">P351</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Remove contact lenses if present and easy to do. continue rinsing">P338</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Get Medical advice/attention if you feel unwell">P314</abbr><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704">NFPA 704</a>
</td>
<td><div style="width:100%; background:transparent;"><div id="refs" style="float:right; padding:1px; text-align:right;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></div><div id="container" style="float:left; margin-left:1em; width:82px; font-family:sans-serif"><div class="nounderlines" id="on_image_elements" style="background:; float:left; font-size:20px; text-align:center; vertical-align:middle; position:relative; height:80px; width:80px; padding:1px;">
<div id="diamond_image_and_mw_ImageMap" style="position:absolute; height:80px; width:80px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_365656039"><area alt="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water" coords="40,0,60,20,40,40,20,20" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" shape="poly" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water"/><area alt="Health code 3: Short exposure could cause serious temporary or residual injury. E.g., chlorine gas" coords="20,20,40,40,20,60,0,40" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" shape="poly" title="Health code 3: Short exposure could cause serious temporary or residual injury. E.g., chlorine gas"/><area alt="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen" coords="60,20,80,40,60,60,40,40" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" shape="poly" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen"/><area alt="Special hazards (white): no code" coords="40,40,60,60,40,80,20,60" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" shape="poly" title="Special hazards (white): no code"/></map><img alt="NFPA 704 four-colored diamond" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/80px-NFPA_704.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/120px-NFPA_704.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/160px-NFPA_704.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_365656039" width="80"/></div></div><div style="width:12px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:12px; left:35px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 3: Short exposure could cause serious temporary or residual injury. E.g., chlorine gas">3</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Elemental iodine (I<sub>2</sub>) is <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxic</a> if taken orally undiluted. The lethal dose for an adult human is 30 mg/kg, which is about 2.1–2.4 grams for a human weighing 70 to 80 kg (even if experiments on rats demonstrated that these animals could survive after eating a 14000 mg/kg dose). Excess iodine can be more <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxic</a> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Selenium_deficiency" title="Selenium deficiency">selenium deficiency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Iodine supplementation in selenium-deficient populations is, in theory, problematic, partly for this reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick2008_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick2008-84">[84]</a></sup> The toxicity derives from its oxidizing properties, through which it denaturates proteins (including enzymes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elemental iodine is also a skin irritant, and direct contact with skin can cause damage and solid iodine crystals should be handled with care. Solutions with high elemental iodine concentration, such as <a href="/wiki/Tincture_of_iodine" title="Tincture of iodine">tincture of iodine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lugol%27s_solution" title="Lugol's solution">Lugol's solution</a>, are capable of causing <a href="/wiki/Dermatoxin" title="Dermatoxin">tissue damage</a> if used in prolonged cleaning or antisepsis; similarly, liquid <a href="/wiki/Povidone-iodine" title="Povidone-iodine">Povidone-iodine</a> (Betadine) trapped against the skin resulted in chemical burns in some reported cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupational_exposure">Occupational exposure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Occupational exposure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>People can be exposed to iodine in the workplace by inhalation, ingestion, skin contact, and eye contact. The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the legal limit (<a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">Permissible exposure limit</a>) for iodine exposure in the workplace at 0.1 ppm (1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>) during an 8-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">Recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 0.1 ppm (1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>) during an 8-hour workday. At levels of 2 ppm, iodine is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergic_reactions">Allergic reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iodine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Allergic reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some people develop a <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitivity" title="Hypersensitivity">hypersensitivity</a> to products and foods containing iodine. Applications of tincture of iodine or Betadine can cause rashes, sometimes severe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dermnet_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-dermnet-108">[108]</a></sup> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/parenteral" title="wikt:parenteral">Parenteral</a> use of iodine-based contrast agents (see above) can cause reactions ranging from a mild rash to fatal <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>. Such reactions have led to the misconception (widely held, even among physicians) that some people are allergic to iodine itself; even allergies to iodine-rich seafood have been so construed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In fact, there has never been a confirmed report of a true iodine allergy, and an allergy to elemental iodine or simple iodide salts is theoretically impossible. Hypersensitivity reactions to products and foods containing iodine are apparently related to their other molecular components;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucsf_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucsf-110">[110]</a></sup> thus, a person who has demonstrated an allergy to one food or product containing iodine may not be have an allergic reaction to another. Patients with various food allergies (shellfish, egg, milk, etc.) or asthma are more likely to suffer reactions to contrast media containing iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucsf_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-ucsf-110">[110]</a></sup>  As with all medications, the patient's allergy history should be questioned and consulted before any containing iodine are administered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Gay-Lussac-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Gay-Lussac_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Gay-Lussac_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Gay-Lussac, J.
